Identifying the Renewers: Al-Sulami's List and Ibn Asakir's Corrections
Lists renewers for five centuries, author adds al-Ghazali and prefers Ash'ari and Baqillani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars like Ibn Asakir evaluated competing claims of who the renewer (mujaddid) was for each century, prioritizing those who defended the Sunnah and had widespread scholarly impact.

Core Idea
Renewers of Islam are identified by their role in defending the Sunnah, not merely by legal expertise.

This passage presents a fascinating scholarly debate about exactly which figures should be counted as the renewers (mujaddids) for the first five centuries of Islam. You will read a report from Abu al-Hasan al-Sulami listing his candidates, then see how the author (Ibn Asakir) interjects his own corrections and preferences. Through this, you'll gain insight into the criteria that classical scholars used to evaluate a scholar's impact on the renewal of religion.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Sulami's list of renewers
Read the Arabic passage noting the five centuries and the names given by al-Sulami: Umar, al-Shafi'i, al-Ash'ari, al-Baqillani, and al-Mustarshid.
This list is the starting point for the debate.
2
Identify the author's correction
Notice where the author says 'But in my opinion' and replaces al-Mustarshid with al-Ghazali for the fifth century, supporting his choice with Ghazali's virtues.
The author prioritizes a scholar's widespread fame and scholarly output.
3
Analyze the alternative views
Read the part where others propose Ibn Surayj for the third century and al-Sa'luki for the fourth, and note how the author rebuts these.
The author judges based on the scholar's role in refuting innovators.
4
Understand the criteria
Reflect on why the author considers al-Ash'ari and al-Baqillani more deserving: their active defense of the Sunnah against the Mu'tazila and other groups.
Impact on preserving correct belief is key.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسمعت الشيخ الامام ابا الحسن علی بن المسلم بن محمد بن على بن الفتح بن على السلمى على كرسيه بجامع دمشق يقول وذكر حديث ابي علقمة هذا فقال : كان على رأس المائة الأولى عمر بن عبد العزيز وكان على رأس المائة الثانية محمد بن ادريس الشافعي وكان على رأس المائة الثالثة الأشعرى وكان على رأس المائة الرابعة ابن الباقلاني وكان على رأس المائة الخامسة امير المؤمنين المسترشد بالله، وعندى ان الذي كان على رأس الخمس مائة الأمام أبو ماجد محمد بن محمد بن محمد بن محمد الغزالي الطوسي الفقيه، لأنه كان عالما عاملا فقيها فاضلا أصوليا كاملا مصنفا عاقلا، انتشر ذكره بالعلم في الآفاق وبرز على من عاصره بخراسان والشام والعراق، وذكر غير الفقيه ابى الحسن ان ابا العباس أحمد ابن عمر بن سريج الفقيه هو الذى كان على رأس الثلثمائة وان ابا الطيب سهل بن محمد بن سليمان الصعلوكي النيسابوري هو الذي كان على . رأس الاربعمائة، وقول من قال انه أبو الحسن الأشعری اصوب لأن قيامه بنصرة السنة إلى تجديد الدين أقرب فهو الذي انتدب للرد على المعتزلة وسائر اصناف المبتدعة المضللة وحالته فى ذلك مشتهرة وكتبه في الرد عليهم منتشرة، فأما أبو العباس بن سريج فكان فقيها مضطلعا بعلم اصول الفقه وفروعه نبيها وقول من قال ان القاضى ابا بكر محمد بن الطيب الباقلاني هو الذى كان على رأس الاربعمائة اولى من القول الثاني لأنه اشهر من أبي الطيب الصعلوكى مكانا وأعلى فى رتب القوم شانا وذكره اكبر من
And I heard the shaykh and imam Abu al-Hasan Ali bin al-Muslim bin Muhammad bin Ali bin al-Fath bin Ali al-Sulami on his pulpit in the Umayyad Mosque of Damascus, saying — and he mentioned this hadith of Abu 'Alqama — and he said: 'At the head of the first century was Umar bin Abd al-Aziz; at the head of the second century was Muhammad bin Idris al-Shafi'i; at the head of the third century was al-Ash'ari; at the head of the fourth century was Ibn al-Baqillani; at the head of the fifth century was the Commander of the Faithful al-Mustarshid bi-Allah.' But in my opinion, the one at the head of five hundred was the imam Abu Majid Muhammad bin Muhammad bin Muhammad bin Muhammad al-Ghazali al-Tusi the jurist, because he was a scholar, a practitioner, a jurist, virtuous, a perfect usuli, a prolific author, intelligent; his fame for knowledge spread to the horizons, and he excelled those of his time in Khurasan, Syria, and Iraq. And someone other than the jurist Abu al-Hasan mentioned that Abu al-Abbas Ahmad bin Umar bin Surayj the jurist was the one at the head of three hundred, and that Abu al-Tayyib Sahl bin Muhammad bin Sulayman al-Sa'luki al-Naysaburi was the one at the head of four hundred. But the statement of those who say that it was Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari is more correct, because his undertaking to support the Sunnah is closer to the renewal of religion; he is the one who undertook to refute the Mu'tazila and all other misguiding innovators, and his state in that is well-known, and his books refuting them are widespread. As for Abu al-Abbas bin Surayj, he was a jurist capable in the science of usul al-fiqh and its branches, insightful. And the statement of those who say that the judge Abu Bakr Muhammad bin al-Tayyib al-Baqillani was the one at the head of four hundred is more worthy than the second statement, because he is more famous than Abu al-Tayyib al-Sa'luki in stature and higher in rank among the people, and his mention is greater than... [text cut off]
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage, from page 54, is a report of a sermon by Abu al-Hasan al-Sulami identifying renewers for the first five centuries: Umar, Shafi'i, Ash'ari, Baqillani, and Caliph al-Mustarshid. The author (likely Ibn Asakir) then interjects his own opinion, correcting the fifth to al-Ghazali, praising his scholarship, practice, and fame. The passage then discusses alternative views: some say Ibn Surayj for the third century and al-Sa'luki for the fourth. The author evaluates these, preferring al-Ash'ari for the third because of his role in defending the Sunnah against the Mu'tazila and other innovators, supported by his well-known works. For the fourth, he prefers al-Baqillani over al-Sa'luki due to his greater stature and fame. The text is cut off, but the argument prioritizes scholars who most directly and demonstrably defended and renewed the Sunnah.

المسترشد بالله
الغزالي
ابن سريج
الصعلوكي
أبو الحسن الأشعري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The list of renewers for each century is fixed and universally agreed upon.
Correction: This passage shows that the identification of renewers involves scholarly debate and personal ijtihad; different scholars have proposed different names based on criteria like defense of the Sunnah and impact.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Ibn Asakir demonstrates that identifying a renewer involves weighing a scholar's contributions to defending the Sunnah and their impact across regions, rather than relying solely on a fixed list.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you spoke up to defend a belief of the Sunnah in a conversation where it was being criticized or mocked? If you cannot recall such a moment, what does that say about your commitment to the truth that the renewers lived and died for? Will you commit to at least one such act this week?
?
Imagine you are evaluated by the same measure Ibn Asakir used for the renewers: your contribution to the defense and spread of authentic Islam. Not your titles or reputation, but your actual impact. Would the verdict be 'more correct' or 'less worthy'? What is the first change you must make today?
?
Al-Ghazali excelled in many fields: fiqh, usul, kalam, tazkiya. You know your own strengths and weaknesses. Which area of Islamic knowledge or practice are you neglecting? Write down one concrete step to develop it, and tell a friend who will hold you accountable by the end of this week.

The Hadith of the Renewer and the First Two Centuries
Hadith of the renewer and identification of first two renewers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadith about a renewer at the head of every century and the identification of the first two renewers by Ahmad ibn Hanbal.

Core Idea
Every century, Allah sends a renewer of religion.

This lesson introduces a well-known hadith about a renewer (mujaddid) appearing at the start of each century to revive religious knowledge. The great imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal transmits this hadith and then gives his own opinion on who the renewers were for the first two centuries: Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz and al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith text
Begin by reading the Arabic hadith and its translation. Notice that the Prophet (peace be upon him) is reported to have promised a renewer for every century.
The chain of narration goes back to Ahmad ibn Hanbal.
2
Identify the first two renewers
Ahmad ibn Hanbal identifies Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz for the first century and al-Shafi'i for the second. Why were these scholars chosen? Consider their roles in reviving the Sunnah and knowledge.
Umar is known for his just caliphate, al-Shafi'i for systematizing Islamic jurisprudence.
3
Reflect on the supplication for al-Shafi'i
Ahmad ibn Hanbal added that he had been supplicating for al-Shafi'i for forty years in his prayers. This shows the deep respect and gratitude he had for al-Shafi'i’s contribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في رأس كل مائة سنة رجلا يعلم الناس دينهم وروى أحمد بن حنبل ذلك عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال أحمد بن حنبل فكان في المائة الأولى عمر بن عبدالعزيز وفي المائة الثانية الشافعي قال أبو عـبـد الله وانا ادعـو للشافعي منذ أربعين سنة في صلاتي اخبرنا الشيخ أبو المظفر أحمد بن الحسين القومسى بها انا جدى لأمى أبو الفضل محمد بن على بن أحمد السهلكي قال حكى الفقيه الصالح الثقة أبو عمرو یعنی محمد بن عبدالله الأديب الرزجاهى قال سمعت الاستاذ الامام ابا سهل الصعلوكي ام الشيخ الامام ابا بكر الاسمعيلى ذكر واحدا والشك منى يقول : اعاد الله تعالى هذا الدين بعد ماذهب يعنى اكثره بأحمد بن حنبل وابي الحسن الأشعري وابي نعيم الاسترابادى وسمعت الشيخ الامام ابا الحسن علی بن المسلم بن محمد بن على بن الفتح بن على السلمى على كرسيه بجامع دمشق يقول وذكر حديث ابي علقمة هذا فقال : كان على رأس المائة الأولى عمر بن عبد العزيز وكان على رأس المائة الثانية محمد بن ادريس الشافعي وكان على رأس المائة الثالثة الأشعرى وكان على رأس المائة الرابعة ابن الباقلاني وكان على رأس المائة الخامسة امير المؤمنين المسترشد بالله، وعندى ان الذي كان على رأس الخمس مائة الأمام أبو ماجد محمد بن محمد بن محمد بن محمد الغزالي الطوسي الفقيه، لأنه كان عالما عاملا فقيها فاضلا أصوليا كاملا مصنفا عاقلا، انتشر ذكره بالعلم في الآفاق وبرز على من عاصره بخراسان والشام والعراق
At the head of every hundred years, a man who teaches the people their religion. Ahmad ibn Hanbal narrated that from the Prophet, peace be upon him. Ahmad ibn Hanbal said: In the first century, it was Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz; in the second century, al-Shafi'i. Abu Abd Allah said: I have been supplicating for al-Shafi'i in my prayer for forty years. Al-Shaykh Abu al-Muzaffar Ahmad ibn al-Husayn al-Qumasi informed us by it: my maternal grandfather Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn Ali ibn Ahmad al-Sahlaki narrated to us: the righteous trustworthy jurist Abu Amr – meaning Muhammad ibn Abd Allah al-Adib al-Razjahi – said: I heard the master Imam Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki, or Imam Abu Bakr al-Isma'ili, mention one – and the doubt is from me – saying: Allah Almighty restored this religion after it had gone, meaning most of it, through Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, and Abu Nu'aym al-Isfarayini. And I heard the Shaykh al-Imam Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn al-Muslim ibn Muhammad ibn Ali ibn al-Fath ibn Ali al-Sulami on his chair in the Damascus Mosque saying – and he mentioned the hadith of Abu 'Alqama – and said: At the head of the first century was Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz; at the head of the second century was Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i; at the head of the third century was al-Ash'ari; at the head of the fourth century was Ibn al-Baqillani; at the head of the fifth century was the Commander of the Faithful al-Mustarshid bi-Allah. And in my view, the one who was at the head of five hundred years is the Imam Abu Majid Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Ghazali al-Tusi al-Faqih, because he was a scholar, a practitioner, a jurist, a virtuous man, a perfect usuli, a prolific author, an intelligent man; his fame spread through knowledge in the horizons, and he excelled over his contemporaries in Khurasan, Syria, and Iraq.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents a tradition (hadith) about a renewer (mujaddid) appearing at the head of every century to teach religion. The author, Ibn Asakir, first cites the narration from Ahmad ibn Hanbal attributing this to the Prophet. Ahmad then identifies Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz for the first century and al-Shafi'i for the second. The author adds a personal note from Abu Abd Allah (likely Ahmad ibn Hanbal himself) that he has been supplicating for al-Shafi'i in prayer for forty years. Then through a chain of transmission, Ibn Asakir reports a statement from Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki or Abu Bakr al-Isma'ili (with doubt from the narrator) that Allah restored the religion through three figures: Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, and Abu Nu'aym al-Isfarayini. Following this, Ibn Asakir transmits from al-Sulami a different list: Umar (1st), al-Shafi'i (2nd), al-Ash'ari (3rd), Ibn al-Baqillani (4th), and al-Mustarshid bi-Allah (5th). Finally, the author gives his own opinion: the one at the head of 500 years (i.e., the sixth century) is Imam al-Ghazali, listing his virtues and widespread reputation. This shows the author's adherence to the mujaddid tradition and his preference for al-Ghazali.

Key Terms
رأس كل مائة سنة
مجدّد
المائة الأولى
المائة الثانية
عمر بن عبدالعزيز
الشافعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith specifies that the renewer must be a single person per century universally agreed upon.
Correction: The hadith mentions a renewer but does not name them; the identification is based on the ijtihad of scholars like Ahmad ibn Hanbal.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The hadith establishes the concept of a renewer for every century, and Ahmad ibn Hanbal identifies Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz and al-Shafi'i as the first two.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Have you personally benefited from his teaching, or are you neglecting the opportunity? Commit today to seeking knowledge from a qualified teacher this week.
?
Ahmad ibn Hanbal made dua for al-Shafi'i for forty years. Think of the scholars who have guided you—do you ever make dua for them? If not, start now. Write down the name of one scholar you can pray for regularly.
?
If a renewer appears to revive religion, then each of us has a responsibility to receive that renewal. What is one area of your religious practice that has become stagnant or neglected? How will you respond to the call of renewal before the next century begins? Take one concrete action today.

The Restoration of Religion Through Three Scholars
Statement that Allah restored religion through three scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how some early scholars held that Allah restored the religion through three key figures: Ahmad ibn Hanbal, al-Ash'ari, and Abu Nu'aym al-Isfarayini.

Core Idea
Allah restored religion through three: Ahmad, Ash'ari, and Abu Nu'aym.

This lesson presents a tradition transmitted by Ibn Asakir from earlier authorities. It states that after most of the religion had disappeared, Allah revived it through three individuals: the great hadith master Ahmad ibn Hanbal, the theologian Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, and the Shafi'i scholar Abu Nu'aym al-Isfarayini. This highlights the importance of these figures in preserving Islamic orthodoxy.

Guided Path
1
Read the transmission chain
Notice the chain of narrators leading back to Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki or Abu Bakr al-Isma'ili. This gives the statement credibility through a scholarly line.
The doubt about the exact narrator shows careful transmission.
2
Identify the three restorers
The statement names Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, and Abu Nu'aym al-Isfarayini. Why are these three linked together? They represent hadith, theology, and jurisprudence respectively.
3
Reflect on the meaning of 'restoration'
The phrase 'after it had gone, meaning most of it' suggests a period of decline. These scholars revived essential aspects of the religion through their works and teachings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

At the head of every hundred years, a man who teaches the people their religion. Ahmad ibn Hanbal narrated that from the Prophet, peace be upon him. Ahmad ibn Hanbal said: In the first century, it was Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz; in the second century, al-Shafi'i. Abu Abd Allah said: I have been supplicating for al-Shafi'i in my prayer for forty years. Al-Shaykh Abu al-Muzaffar Ahmad ibn al-Husayn al-Qumasi informed us by it: my maternal grandfather Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn Ali ibn Ahmad al-Sahlaki narrated to us: the righteous trustworthy jurist Abu Amr – meaning Muhammad ibn Abd Allah al-Adib al-Razjahi – said: I heard the master Imam Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki, or Imam Abu Bakr al-Isma'ili, mention one – and the doubt is from me – saying: Allah Almighty restored this religion after it had gone, meaning most of it, through Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, and Abu Nu'aym al-Isfarayini. And I heard the Shaykh al-Imam Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn al-Muslim ibn Muhammad ibn Ali ibn al-Fath ibn Ali al-Sulami on his chair in the Damascus Mosque saying – and he mentioned the hadith of Abu 'Alqama – and said: At the head of the first century was Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz; at the head of the second century was Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i; at the head of the third century was al-Ash'ari; at the head of the fourth century was Ibn al-Baqillani; at the head of the fifth century was the Commander of the Faithful al-Mustarshid bi-Allah. And in my view, the one who was at the head of five hundred years is the Imam Abu Majid Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Ghazali al-Tusi al-Faqih, because he was a scholar, a practitioner, a jurist, a virtuous man, a perfect usuli, a prolific author, an intelligent man; his fame spread through knowledge in the horizons, and he excelled over his contemporaries in Khurasan, Syria, and Iraq.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents a tradition (hadith) about a renewer (mujaddid) appearing at the head of every century to teach religion. The author, Ibn Asakir, first cites the narration from Ahmad ibn Hanbal attributing this to the Prophet. Ahmad then identifies Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz for the first century and al-Shafi'i for the second. The author adds a personal note from Abu Abd Allah (likely Ahmad ibn Hanbal himself) that he has been supplicating for al-Shafi'i in prayer for forty years. Then through a chain of transmission, Ibn Asakir reports a statement from Abu Sahl al-Sa'luki or Abu Bakr al-Isma'ili (with doubt from the narrator) that Allah restored the religion through three figures: Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, and Abu Nu'aym al-Isfarayini. Following this, Ibn Asakir transmits from al-Sulami a different list: Umar (1st), al-Shafi'i (2nd), al-Ash'ari (3rd), Ibn al-Baqillani (4th), and al-Mustarshid bi-Allah (5th). Finally, the author gives his own opinion: the one at the head of 500 years (i.e., the sixth century) is Imam al-Ghazali, listing his virtues and widespread reputation. This shows the author's adherence to the mujaddid tradition and his preference for al-Ghazali.

Key Terms
أحمد بن حنبل
أبو الحسن الأشعري
أبو نعيم الاسترابادى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only one person can restore the religion at a time.
Correction: The statement shows that multiple scholars can play a role, each in a different field.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three scholars through whom Allah restored the religion according to this passage?
A) Ahmad ibn Hanbal
B) Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari
C) Abu Nu'aym al-Isfarayini
D) Al-Shafi'i

Key Takeaway
Allah revived the religion through the combined efforts of Ahmad ibn Hanbal (hadith), al-Ash'ari (theology), and Abu Nu'aym (jurisprudence).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson tells you that Allah restored the religion through three scholars representing hadith, theology, and jurisprudence. Which of these three areas do you find yourself neglecting most in your daily practice? What is one concrete action you will take this week to strengthen that area?
?
If the religion was restored through the efforts of these scholars, are you contributing to its preservation in your time? Think of one thing you do – or fail to do – that either strengthens or weakens the religion around you. What will you change starting today?
?
The chain of narrators in this passage includes a doubt about who said the statement. Does your own transmission of Islamic knowledge come with the same level of accuracy and care? How often do you check your sources before passing on religious information? Commit to one change in how you verify knowledge.

The Ash'ari Chain of Renewers and al-Ghazali
List of renewers up to fifth century and Ibn Asakir's choice of al-Ghazali for the sixth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the list of renewers provided by al-Sulami (including al-Ash'ari, al-Baqillani, and al-Mustarshid) and Ibn Asakir's opinion that al-Ghazali was the renewer at the head of the sixth century.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali is the renewer of the sixth century in Ibn Asakir's view.

This lesson presents another list of renewers, transmitted by al-Sulami, which includes al-Ash'ari, al-Baqillani, and the Abbasid caliph al-Mustarshid for the first five centuries. Following that, Ibn Asakir gives his own considered opinion: the renewer at the head of the sixth century (500 AH) is Imam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, whom he praises with multiple attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the list from al-Sulami
Al-Sulami gives a sequence: Umar, al-Shafi'i, al-Ash'ari, al-Baqillani, and al-Mustarshid. Notice how it shifts from caliphs to scholars and back to a caliph.
2
Compare with the earlier lists
Compare this list with the one from Ahmad ibn Hanbal. What is similar? What is different? Al-Ash'ari and al-Baqillani are new additions.
This list extends to the fifth century.
3
Examine Ibn Asakir's opinion on al-Ghazali
Ibn Asakir asserts that al-Ghazali is the renewer at 500 AH. He lists his virtues: scholar, practitioner, jurist, virtuous, usuli, author, intelligent. This shows the high esteem in which al-Ghazali was held.
Al-Ghazali died in 505 AH, so this fits chronologically.

Biography of Abu Nu'aym al-Isfarayini
Ibn Asakir transmits biographical data on Abu Nu'aym via a chain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the biographical details of Abu Nu'aym al-Isfarayini as transmitted by Ibn Asakir, including his scholarly status and death date.

Core Idea
Chains of transmission preserve the trustworthiness of historical reports.

Ibn Asakir begins his biographical entry on Abu Nu'aym al-Isfarayini by citing a chain of narrators leading to al-Khatib al-Baghdadi. This chain establishes the authority of the information about Abu Nu'aym's life and scholarship. Let's explore what is said about him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the chain of narrators at the beginning.
The chain includes al-Khatib al-Baghdadi.
2
Identify Abu Nu'aym's identity
Find the name and lineage: Abd al-Malik al-Jurjani al-Faqih. Note his title 'al-Isfarayini' refers to his origin.
3
Note the qualities attributed
Al-Khatib describes him as an Imam, Hafiz, with truthfulness, piety, precision, and vigilance. He traveled widely and wrote in many regions.
4
Record his death date
He died around 320 AH. The author adds that he supported the Sunnah in Jurjan.
The date is approximate ('around').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاما أبو نعيم الاستراباذي فهو عبدالملك الجرجاني الفقيه، قال لنا أبو الحسين على بن أحمد بن منصور الغساني بدمشق وأبو منصور عبد الرحمن بن محمد بن عبد الواحد بن زريق الشيباني ببغداد، قال لنا أبو بكر أحمد بن على بن ثابت الخطيب إنه كان أحد أئمة المسلمين ومن الحفاظ في الشرائع والدين مع الحفاظ فى الشرائع والدين مع صدق وتورع وضبط وتيقظ، سافر الكثير وكتب بالعراق والحجاز والشام ومصر ومات حدود سنة عشرين وثلثمائة قلت وكان ينصر السنة بجرجان فاما عمر بن عبد العزيز بن مروان بن الحكم بن ابی العاص ابن زمـيـة بن عبد شمس وكانت وفاته كما أخبرنا الشيخ أبو غالب أحمد بن الحسن بن أحمد بن البناء ببغداد قال انا أبو الحسين محمد بن أحمد بن محمد ابن علی بن الأبنوسى انا أبو القسم عبد الله بن عثمان بن يحيى بن خبيق الدقاق، انا أبو محمد اسمعیل بن علی بن اسماعيل الخطبى قال اخبرنی محمد بن موسى بن حماد البربرى عن محمد بن ابى السرى ان عمر بن عبدالعزيز توفى لأربع ليال يعني من رجب سنة احدى ومائة وهو ابن تسع وثلاثين سنة ونصف، قال ابن ابى السرى قال العمرى توفى يوم الجمعة لخمس ليال بقين من رجب وقبره بدير سمعان وكانت ولايته سنتين وخمسة أشهر وخمسة أيام، وأما الشافعى فكانت وفاته فيما اخبرنا الشيخ الفقيه أبو الحسن على بن المسلم السلمى انا أبو نصر الحسين بن محمد بن
As for Abu Nu'aym al-Isfarayini, he is Abd al-Malik al-Jurjani al-Faqih. Abu al-Husayn Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Mansur al-Ghassani in Damascus and Abu Mansur Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahid ibn Zurayq al-Shaybani in Baghdad said to us: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib said to us: He was one of the Imams of the Muslims and among the memorizers of the laws and religion, with truthfulness, piety, precision, and vigilance. He traveled extensively and wrote in Iraq, Hijaz, Syria, and Egypt, and he died around the year 320. I say: He was supporting the Sunnah in Jurjan. As for Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz ibn Marwan ibn al-Hakam ibn Abi al-As ibn Umayya ibn Abd Shams, his death was as informed by Shaykh Abu Ghalib Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn Ahmad ibn al-Banna in Baghdad: Abu al-Husayn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Ali ibn al-Abnusi narrated to us: Abu al-Qasim Abd Allah ibn Uthman ibn Yahya ibn Khubayq al-Daqqaq narrated to us: Abu Muhammad Isma'il ibn Ali ibn Isma'il al-Khatabi narrated to us: Muhammad ibn Musa ibn Hammad al-Barbari informed me from Muhammad ibn Abi al-Sari: that Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz died on the fourth night (meaning of Rajab) in the year 101, and he was 39 and a half years old. Ibn Abi al-Sari said: Al-Umari said: He died on Friday, five nights remaining of Rajab, and his grave is in Dayr Sim'an, and his reign was two years, five months, and five days. As for al-Shafi'i, his death was as informed by the Shaykh al-Faqih Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn al-Muslim al-Sulami: Abu Nasr al-Husayn ibn Muhammad ibn...
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage provides biographical summaries of three important figures. First, Abu Nu'aym al-Isfarayini: the author identifies him as Abd al-Malik al-Jurjani al-Faqih, citing al-Khatib al-Baghdadi's praise that he was an Imam and a Hafiz in religious sciences, known for truthfulness and precision. He traveled widely and died around 320 AH. The author adds that he supported the Sunnah in Jurjan. Second, Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz: the author details his lineage and then gives a precise account of his death through a chain of narration. He died in Rajab 101 AH, at age 39.5, after a reign of 2 years, 5 months, and 5 days, and was buried in Dayr Sim'an. Third, al-Shafi'i: the passage begins his death notice but is cut off at page end. The chain includes Abu al-Hasan al-Sulami narrating from Abu Nasr al-Husayn. This shows Ibn Asakir's method of transmitting biographical data with full chains.

Key Terms
أبو نعيم الاستراباذي
الخطيب البغدادي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The chains of narration are just a formality and not essential for historical trust.
Correction: Chains are crucial for verifying the authenticity and transmission of reports; they connect the report back to reliable authorities.

Checkpoint

A) 310 AH
B) 320 AH

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are your comforts hindering your pursuit of truth? Commit to one concrete change in your daily routine to prioritize Islamic knowledge, even if it costs you convenience.
?
Al-Khatib al-Baghdadi testified that Abu Nu'aym had 'truthfulness, piety, precision, and vigilance.' Which of these qualities are you most lacking in your own learning and practice? Be honest. Write down a specific action you can take this week to strengthen that quality, and ask someone to hold you accountable.
?
The chain of narrators in this passage is what makes the report reliable. Think about the sources of your own religious knowledge: do you even know who transmitted them? How can you accept a teaching without verifying its chain? Starting today, resolve to trace every important religious claim back to its source, and if you cannot, humbly admit you do not know.

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir

Biography of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz
Ibn Asakir records the death details of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz with a full chain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the precise death date, age, reign length, and burial place of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz as recorded by Ibn Asakir through a chain of narration.

Core Idea
Every detail of a pious caliph's life is preserved through trustworthy chains.

Ibn Asakir provides a detailed account of the death of the Umayyad caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz. Through a chain of narrators, he gives the exact date, his age, and the length of his caliphate. This precision reflects the care historians take in preserving important events.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the full lineage
Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz ibn Marwan ibn al-Hakam etc. This establishes his Umayyad genealogy.
2
Trace the chain of narration
Note the narrators: from Ibn al-Banna to al-Khatabi to Muhammad ibn Abi al-Sari. The chain is long, indicating careful transmission.
The final authority is Muhammad ibn Abi al-Sari.
3
Extract the key numerical details
Death: Rajab 101 AH, age 39.5, reign 2 years 5 months 5 days. Burial: Dayr Sim'an.
Two slightly different reports are given for the exact day.
4
Reflect on the importance of these details
Such precision allows historians to cross-check events. It also honors the legacy of a caliph known for justice.

As for Abu Nu'aym al-Isfarayini, he is Abd al-Malik al-Jurjani al-Faqih. Abu al-Husayn Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Mansur al-Ghassani in Damascus and Abu Mansur Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahid ibn Zurayq al-Shaybani in Baghdad said to us: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib said to us: He was one of the Imams of the Muslims and among the memorizers of the laws and religion, with truthfulness, piety, precision, and vigilance. He traveled extensively and wrote in Iraq, Hijaz, Syria, and Egypt, and he died around the year 320. I say: He was supporting the Sunnah in Jurjan. As for Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz ibn Marwan ibn al-Hakam ibn Abi al-As ibn Umayya ibn Abd Shams, his death was as informed by Shaykh Abu Ghalib Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn Ahmad ibn al-Banna in Baghdad: Abu al-Husayn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Ali ibn al-Abnusi narrated to us: Abu al-Qasim Abd Allah ibn Uthman ibn Yahya ibn Khubayq al-Daqqaq narrated to us: Abu Muhammad Isma'il ibn Ali ibn Isma'il al-Khatabi narrated to us: Muhammad ibn Musa ibn Hammad al-Barbari informed me from Muhammad ibn Abi al-Sari: that Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz died on the fourth night (meaning of Rajab) in the year 101, and he was 39 and a half years old. Ibn Abi al-Sari said: Al-Umari said: He died on Friday, five nights remaining of Rajab, and his grave is in Dayr Sim'an, and his reign was two years, five months, and five days. As for al-Shafi'i, his death was as informed by the Shaykh al-Faqih Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn al-Muslim al-Sulami: Abu Nasr al-Husayn ibn Muhammad ibn...
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage provides biographical summaries of three important figures. First, Abu Nu'aym al-Isfarayini: the author identifies him as Abd al-Malik al-Jurjani al-Faqih, citing al-Khatib al-Baghdadi's praise that he was an Imam and a Hafiz in religious sciences, known for truthfulness and precision. He traveled widely and died around 320 AH. The author adds that he supported the Sunnah in Jurjan. Second, Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz: the author details his lineage and then gives a precise account of his death through a chain of narration. He died in Rajab 101 AH, at age 39.5, after a reign of 2 years, 5 months, and 5 days, and was buried in Dayr Sim'an. Third, al-Shafi'i: the passage begins his death notice but is cut off at page end. The chain includes Abu al-Hasan al-Sulami narrating from Abu Nasr al-Husayn. This shows Ibn Asakir's method of transmitting biographical data with full chains.

Key Terms
عمر بن عبد العزيز
دير سمعان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The precise numbers are likely later fabrications.
Correction: The detailed numbers are transmitted via chains to contemporaries, showing careful record-keeping.

Checkpoint

B) 101 AH
C) 102 AH
D) 99 AH

Key Takeaway
The death and reign of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz are recorded with exact dates through a reliable chain of narration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The chain of narrators took great care to transmit this information accurately. How carefully do you transmit knowledge about Islam to others? Do you check your sources, or do you repeat things without verification?
?
Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz's reign was only two years, five months, and five days, yet it left a lasting legacy. If your life were summarized by a single period of responsibility, what would that summary say? And will you be content with it on the Day of Judgment?

A Family's Defense of Ash'arism in Nishapur
Ibn Asakir describes the role of Abu at-Tayyib's family in spreading Ash'arism against innovators in Nishapur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the family of Abu at-Tayyib actively propagated Ash'arism in Nishapur and refuted accusations, while defending tawhid against three innovating sects.

Core Idea
A single family championed Ash'arism against three sects: Mu'tazila, Rafida, Karramiyya.

Ibn Asakir presents a biographical vignette that demonstrates Ash'arism was not merely a theoretical school but a living tradition defended by successive generations. This passage highlights the active struggle of the family of Abu at-Tayyib in Nishapur, showing how they manifested the Ash'ari school and combated innovation. Read on to see how their legacy is used to refute false accusations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three groups of innovators
Read the Arabic carefully to find the three sects mentioned: al-Mu'tazila, al-Rafida, al-Karramiyya. Notice how the family fought against all of them.
These are three distinct groups that opposed Ash'arism.
2
Notice the multigenerational commitment
The passage mentions Abu at-Tayyib, his father Abu Sahl, and his son (grandson) – three generations consistently defending Ash'arism.
3
Understand the refutation of accusation
The passage connects their well-known defense of tawhid and tanzih to disprove a false accusation by Abu Ali al-Ahwazi.
This shows how scholarship and character refute calumny.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أكثر من محمد بن [بن؟] عدی ان ينكر وقدره أظهر من ان يستر وتصانيفه اشهر من ان تشهر وتواليفه ان تذكر، فاما أبو الطيب رحمه الله فانما اشتهر ذكره ببلده وكانت رياسة اصحاب الشافعي له بنيسابور ولوالده ولولده وكان ابوه أبو سهل محمد بن سليمان رحمه الله ذا محل خطير، وذكره فيما بين أهل العلم خراسان كبير لم يزل هو وولده وولد ولده يظهرون مذهب الأشعرية ويجاهدون اهل البدع بنيسابور من المعتزلة والرافضة والكرامية، وما تقدم من قوله في مدح الأشعرى مما رواه عنه أبو عمرو الرزجاهي يدل على كذب ابي على الاهوازي فيما حكى عنه اذ رماه باحدى الدواهي مع ما اشتهر عنه بخراسان من الذب عن أهل التوحيد وتنزيه الرب عز وجل عن التشبيه والتحديد مقتديا بالأشعرى وسالكا طريقه مقتفيا في علم الأصول نهجه وتحقيقه.
More than Muhammad ibn [ibn?] Adi can deny, and his rank is too apparent to be concealed, and his books are more famous than to be renowned, and his compilations are mentioned. As for Abu at-Tayyib, may God have mercy on him, his fame was only in his town, and the leadership of the Shafi'i scholars in Nishapur belonged to him, his father, and his son. His father Abu Sahl Muhammad ibn Sulayman, may God have mercy on him, was of high standing. His mention among the people of knowledge in Khorasan was great. He, his son, and his grandson never ceased to manifest the Ash'ari school and to struggle against the people of innovation in Nishapur from the Mu'tazila, Rafida, and Karramiyya. What preceded of his words in praise of al-Ash'ari, narrated from him by Abu Amr al-Razjahi, indicates the falsehood of Abu Ali al-Ahwazi in what he reported about him, when he accused him of one of the calamities, besides what is well-known about him in Khorasan of defending the people of tawhid and exalting the Lord, Mighty and Majestic, from anthropomorphism and limitation, following al-Ash'ari, traversing his path, and following his method and verification in the science of principles.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a biographical notice about a family of scholars in Nishapur who championed the Ash'ari school. The author begins by praising someone (perhaps a scholar named Muhammad ibn Adi) whose works are too famous to need mention. Then he focuses on Abu at-Tayyib, a Shafi'i leader whose family inherited the leadership of the Shafi'is in Nishapur. He notes that Abu at-Tayyib's father, Abu Sahl, was highly respected. The family actively manifested the Ash'ari madhhab and struggled against innovators: the Mu'tazila, Rafida (Shi'a), and Karramiyya. The author then refers to a previous statement praising al-Ash'ari, transmitted by Abu Amr al-Razjahi, which disproves a false accusation by Abu Ali al-Ahwazi against this scholar. Finally, he emphasizes the scholar's well-known defense of tawhid and divine transcendence (tanzih) from anthropomorphism (tashbih) and limitation (tahdid), following the path of al-Ash'ari in usul al-din.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the scholar (Abu at-Tayyib's family) supported anthropomorphism (tashbih) or limitation (tahdid) of Allah.
Correction: The passage states they were well-known for exalting Allah from tashbih and tahdid, defending tawhid, and following al-Ash'ari.

Checkpoint
Which three groups of innovators did the family of Abu at-Tayyib struggle against in Nishapur?
A) Mu'tazila, B) Rafida, C) Karramiyya, D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school was actively defended by a family of Shafi'i scholars in Nishapur against Mu'tazila, Rafida, and Karramiyya, refuting false accusations through their consistent defense of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own context, are you willing to stand up for your beliefs even if it means a long struggle against multiple opposed groups, as the family of Abu at-Tayyib did? What specific compromise or silence are you tempted by that goes against tawhid?
?
The passage shows that the family's defense was rooted in exalting God from anthropomorphism. Have you ever imagined God in human terms? What step will you take today to purify your concept of tawhid based on this lesson?
?
A false accusation was refuted by the scholar's consistent record. When people misunderstand or slander you, does your life speak clearly enough to refute it? What area of your character needs greater consistency so that your actions match your claimed beliefs?

Biography of Abu Nu'aym al-Istarabadi
Ibn Asakir provides a biographical notice on Abu Nu'aym al-Istarabadi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key biographical details and virtues of the hadith scholar Abu Nu'aym al-Istarabadi.

Core Idea
Abu Nu'aym's life exemplifies the pious dedication of early hadith scholars.

This lesson presents a brief biography of Abu Nu'aym al-Istarabadi, a prominent hadith memorizer and supporter of the Sunni tradition, as recorded by Ibn Asakir. The passage highlights his learning journey, scholarly reputation, and his role in upholding the Sunna.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to understand the overall content.
Focus on the chain of transmission and the description of his qualities.
2
Identify the chain and key qualities
Trace the narration from the author back to al-Khatib al-Baghdadi. Notice the virtues attributed: truthfulness, piety, precision, vigilance.
3
Note the author's personal testimony
The author ends with 'I say: He used to support the Sunna in Jurjan.' This shows his own endorsement and the regional context.
This connects to the broader theme of upholding the Sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاما أبو نعيم الاستراباذي فهو عبدالملك الجرجاني الفقيه، قال لنا أبو الحسين على بن أحمد بن منصور الغساني بدمشق وأبو منصور عبد الرحمن بن محمد بن عبد الواحد بن زريق الشيباني ببغداد، قال لنا أبو بكر أحمد بن على بن ثابت الخطيب إنه كان أحد أئمة المسلمين ومن الحفاظ في الشرائع والدين مع الحفاظ في الشرائع والدين مع صدق وتورع وضبط وتيقظ، سافر الكثير وكتب بالعراق والحجاز والشام ومصر ومات حدود سنة عشرين وثلثمائة قلت وكان ينصر السنة بجرجان.
As for Abu Nu'aym al-Istarabadi, he is Abd al-Malik al-Jurjani the faqih. He said to us: Abu al-Husayn Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Mansur al-Ghassani in Damascus and Abu Mansur Abd al-Rahman ibn Muhammad ibn Abd al-Wahid ibn Zurayq al-Shaybani in Baghdad. They said to us: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ali ibn Thabit al-Khatib [said]: He was one of the imams of the Muslims and among the memorizers of the shar'i sciences and religion, with truthfulness, piety, precision, and vigilance. He traveled extensively and wrote in Iraq, Hijaz, Syria, and Egypt, and he died around the year 320 AH. I say: He used to support the Sunna in Jurjan.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a biographical notice about Abu Nu'aym (Abd al-Malik al-Jurjani), praising his stature as a hadith memorizer and his travels to collect knowledge. The author cites a chain of narrators to al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, who attested to his qualities. The author adds his own comment that Abu Nu'aym supported the Sunna in Jurjan.

Correction: They refer to the same person; he was from Istarbad, a town near Jurjan.

Death Date of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz
Ibn Asakir records the death of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz with chain of narration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific details of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz's death date, age, burial place, and length of his caliphate as recorded by Ibn Asakir.

Core Idea
Historical precision honors the legacy of a righteous leader.

Ibn Asakir, through a meticulous chain of narration, provides us with precise details about the death of Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, the Umayyad caliph known for his piety and reform. This passage not only gives us his death date and age but also the location of his grave and the exact duration of his rule. Such specificity reflects the care with which Muslim scholars preserved biographical data.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation side by side. Notice the chain of narrators that Ibn Asakir cites.
The chain shows the scholarly effort to authenticate the report.
2
Identify the key factual details
Look for four pieces of information: the date of death (month, year, and day), his age at death, the location of his grave, and the length of his caliphate.
There are two slightly different reports about the exact day.
3
Reflect on the significance of the details
Consider why scholars recorded such precise details about the deaths of important figures. This precision helps establish historical timelines and shows respect for their legacy.
The length of his rule is given in years, months, and days.
4
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question about the duration of his caliphate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاما عمر بن عبد العزيز بن مروان بن الحكم بن ابی العاص ابن زمـيــة بن عبد شمس وكانت وفاته كما أخبرنا الشيخ أبو غالب أحمد بن الحسن بن أحمد بن البناء ببغداد قال انا أبو الحسين محمد بن أحمد بن محمد ابن علی بن الأبنوسى انا أبو القاسم عبد الله بن عثمان بن يحيى بن خبيق الدقاق، انا أبو محمد إسماعيل بن علی بن إسماعيل الخطبي قال أخبرني محمد بن موسى بن حماد البربري عن محمد بن أبي السرى ان عمر بن عبد العزيز توفى لأربع ليال يعني من رجب سنة إحدى ومائة وهو ابن تسع وثلاثين سنة ونصف، قال ابن أبي السرى قال العمري توفى يوم الجمعة لخمس ليال بقين من رجب وقبره بدير سمعان وكانت ولايته سنتين وخمسة أشهر وخمسة أيام.
As for Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz ibn Marwan ibn al-Hakam ibn Abi al-As ibn Umayya ibn Abd Shams, his death was as informed to us by Shaykh Abu Ghalib Ahmad ibn al-Hasan ibn Ahmad ibn al-Banna in Baghdad, he said: Inna Abu al-Husayn Muhammad ibn Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Ali ibn al-Abnusi: Inna Abu al-Qasim Abd Allah ibn Uthman ibn Yahya ibn Khabiq al-Daqqaq: Inna Abu Muhammad Isma'il ibn Ali ibn Isma'il al-Khatbi: He told me Muhammad ibn Musa ibn Hammad al-Barbari from Muhammad ibn Abi al-Sari that Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz died with four nights remaining, meaning of Rajab, in the year 101 AH, and he was thirty-nine and a half years old. Ibn Abi al-Sari said: al-Amri said: He died on Friday with five nights remaining of Rajab, and his grave is in Dayr Sam'an. His governorship was two years, five months, and five days.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a biographical notice on Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, providing his death date through an extensive chain of narration. The author gives two slightly different reports about the exact day but confirms the year and his age. He also notes the length of his caliphate (two years, five months, five days) and the location of his grave (Dayr Sam'an).

Key Terms
عمر بن عبد العزيز
دير سمعان
ولاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Two years, five months, five days
C) Three years
D) Two years exactly

Key Takeaway
Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz died in Rajab 101 AH at age 39.5, was buried at Dayr Sam'an, and his caliphate lasted 2 years, 5 months, and 5 days.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz died at 39.5 years old, having led the Islamic caliphate for just over 2.5 years. ?

Are you equally careful in preserving the truth when you transmit news about others, or do you allow carelessness or bias to alter what you pass on? What specific step will you take today to ensure your speech is as precise as this chain?

Death Date of Imam al-Shafi'i (204 AH)
Ibn Asakir records the death of Imam al-Shafi'i with a chain of narration from al-Rabi' ibn Sulayman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise death date and month of Imam al-Shafi'i as recorded by Ibn Asakir through a reliable chain of narration.

Core Idea
A direct student reports the exact death date of Imam al-Shafi'i.

In this passage, Ibn Asakir provides a specific historical detail about Imam al-Shafi'i: the date of his death. He cites a chain of narrators ending with al-Rabi' ibn Sulayman, a close student of al-Shafi'i, who personally states the year and month. This brief but important notice clarifies a key biographical fact and demonstrates the care taken by scholars to preserve such information accurately.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the chain of names and the final statement about the death date.
The chain ensures the reliability of the report.
2
Identify the direct source
The last narrator, al-Rabi' ibn Sulayman, says 'I heard' (سمعت) – he is the direct student who witnessed or heard the date directly.
Al-Rabi' was a student and companion of al-Shafi'i.
3
Note the specific date
Al-Shafi'i died in the year 204 AH at the end of the month of Rajab (آخر رجب). This is more precise than simply saying 204 AH.
Some historical reports only mention the year.
4
Understand the chain's significance
The chain of narrators (Ibn Asakir's chain back to al-Rabi') shows how biographical knowledge was transmitted and verified.
Each link in the chain is named and known, affirming authenticity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشافعي فكانت وفاته فيما أخبرنا الشيخ الفقيه أبو الحسن علي بن المسلم السلمي: أنا أبو نصر الحسين بن محمد بن [حمد بن؟] طلاب الخطيب بدمشق: أنبأنا أبو بكر محمد بن عثمان بن أبي الحديد السلمي: أنا أبو بكر محمد بن بشر الزبيري العسكري بمصر قال: سمعت الربيع بن سليمان يقول: مات الشافعي في سنة أربع ومائتين في آخر رجب.
As for al-Shafi'i, his death was as informed to us by al-Shaykh al-Faqih Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn al-Muslim al-Sulami: Inna Abu Nasr al-Husayn ibn Muhammad ibn [Hammad?] Talab al-Khatib in Damascus: Anba'ana Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Uthman ibn Abi al-Hadid al-Sulami: Inna Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn Bishr al-Zubayri al-Askari in Egypt said: I heard al-Rabi' ibn Sulayman say: Al-Shafi'i died in the year 204 AH at the end of Rajab.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a brief biographical notice giving the death date of Imam al-Shafi'i. The author provides a chain of narration ending with al-Rabi' ibn Sulayman, a direct student of al-Shafi'i, who states that al-Shafi'i died in 204 AH at the end of the month of Rajab. The chain confirms the reliability of the report.

Correction: This narration specifies he died at the end of Rajab, providing a more precise date.

?

?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir

The Death and Conversion of al-Ash'ari: Clarifying the Dates
Ibn Asakir determines al-Ash'ari's death date and clarifies the conversion date.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct death date of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari according to Ibn Asakir and why the year 300 AH is often mistakenly associated with his death.

Core Idea
Al-Ash'ari died in 324 AH, not 300 AH — the latter is the year of his conversion.

Ibn Asakir addresses a common confusion about the date of al-Ash'ari's death. Several reports exist, but the author weighs them and clarifies that the year 300 AH refers to his return to Sunni Islam, not his passing. This lesson walks through the evidence and the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Carefully read the full Arabic passage and the English translation. Notice the different dates mentioned and the author's commentary on each.
Pay attention to the chain of transmission at the beginning.
2
Identify the reported death dates
List the different death dates mentioned in the passage: between 320-330 AH, 324 AH, and 330-something. Note which one the author affirms.
The author explicitly states 'الأصح أنه مات سنة أربع وعشرين وثلاثمائة'.
3
Understand the correction about 300 AH
Note that the author explains the significance of the date 300 AH: it refers to al-Ash'ari's return to Ahl al-Sunna, not his death. This is a key clarification.
The phrase 'فيكون التاريخ سنة ثلاثمائة لرجوعه' makes this clear.
4
Connect the biographical context
The passage notes that al-Ash'ari's conversion occurred during the lifetime of his teacher al-Jubba'i, who died in 303 AH. This supports the conversion date of 300 AH.
This shows that the conversion happened before al-Jubba'i's death.
5
Reflect on the confusion
Consider why people might confuse the conversion date with the death date. The passage helps resolve this by providing clear evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما وفاة أبي الحسن الأشعري فأخبرنا الشيخان أبو الحسن علي بن أحمد المالكي وأبو منصور محمد بن عبد الملك المقرئ قالا قال لنا أبو بكر أحمد بن علي الحافظ ذكر لي أبو القاسم عبد الواحد بن علي الأسدي أن الأشعري مات ببغداد بعد سنة عشرين وقبل سنة ثلاثين وثلاثمائة ودفن في مشرع الزوايا في تربة إلى جانبها مسجد وبالقرب منها حمام وهي عن يسار المار من السوق إلى دجلة وذكر أبو محمد علي بن أحمد ابن سعيد بن حزم الأندلسي أن أبا الحسن الأشعري مات سنة أربع وعشرين وثلاثمائة وقال بعض العصريين مات سنة نيف وثلاثين وثلاثمائة وهذا القول الأخير لا أراه صحيحا والأصح أنه مات سنة أربع وعشرين وكذلك ذكر أبو بكر بن فورك فيكون التاريخ سنة ثلاثمائة لرجوعه إلى مذهب أهل السنة لا للوقت الذي فيه هلك، وكان رجوعه في حياة الجبائي أبي علي وجداله إياه بعد رجوعه من الأمر الجلي، وكانت وفاة الجبائي كما ذكر بعض أهل الاتقان في سنة ثلاث وثلاثمائة في شعبان.
As for the death of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the two shaykhs Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Ahmad al-Maliki and Abu Mansur Muhammad ibn Abd al-Malik al-Muqri' informed us, they said: Abu Bakr Ahmad ibn Ali al-Hafiz told us: Abu al-Qasim Abd al-Wahid ibn Ali al-Asadi mentioned to me that al-Ash'ari died in Baghdad after the year 320 and before 330 AH, and was buried in Mashra' al-Zawaya in a grave next to which is a mosque and near it a bathhouse, and it is to the left of one passing from the market to the Tigris. Abu Muhammad Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Sa'id ibn Hazm al-Andalusi mentioned that Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari died in the year 324 AH. Some contemporaries said he died in the year 330-something AH. I do not consider this last statement correct. The correct view is that he died in 324 AH. Likewise, Abu Bakr ibn Furak mentioned it. Thus the date [of his death] is 300 AH for his return to the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna, not for the time when he died. His return was during the lifetime of al-Jubba'i, Abu Ali, and his debate with him after his return from the clear matter. The death of al-Jubba'i, as some men of precision mentioned, was in the year 303 AH in Sha'ban.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the death date of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari. The author cites multiple reports: one that he died between 320 and 330 AH, another from Ibn Hazm that it was 324 AH, and a third from contemporaries that it was 330-something. The author rejects the latest estimate and affirms 324 AH, supported by Ibn Furak. He then clarifies that the date 300 AH often associated with al-Ash'ari actually refers to his conversion to Sunni Islam, not his death. He adds biographical context: al-Ash'ari's return occurred during the lifetime of his teacher al-Jubba'i, with whom he debated after his clear departure from Mu'tazilism. The death of al-Jubba'i is given as 303 AH in Sha'ban.

Key Terms
أبو الحسن الأشعري
الجبائي
الرجوع
أهل السنة
مشرع الزوايا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think al-Ash'ari died in the year 300 AH.
Correction: In reality, 300 AH is the year he returned to Sunni Islam; he died in 324 AH.

Checkpoint

A) 300 AH
B) 303 AH
C) 324 AH
D) 330 AH

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari passed away in 324 AH, while his return to the Sunni path occurred in 300 AH, during the lifetime of al-Jubba'i.

Death Date of al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani
Ibn Asakir records the death of the Ash'ari theologian al-Baqillani in 403 AH.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the precise date of al-Baqillani's death as recorded by Ibn Asakir, including the day, month, and year.

Core Idea
Al-Baqillani died on a Saturday in 403 AH, as transmitted through a reliable chain.

This lesson presents a brief but important biographical snippet from Ibn Asakir's Tabyin. Knowing the death dates of major scholars helps us situate their work in history. Here, we learn the exact day, month, and year of al-Baqillani's passing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage with its literal translation. Observe the chain of narrators and the date information.
The chain shows how the information was transmitted.
2
Identify the chain of narration
Notice the names: Ibn Asakir receives from Abu al-Hasan Ali... who from Abu Bakr Ahmad... who from Ali ibn Abi Ali al-Mu'addil, who directly reports the death.
Each link adds reliability.
3
Extract the date components
Find the day (Saturday), the month (Dhu al-Qa'da), the day of month (seven days remaining, i.e., 23rd), and the year (403 AH).
This precise dating helps in historical chronology.

Source Text

وأما وفاة القاضي أبي بكر بن الطيب الباقلاني فأخبرنا أبو الحسن علي بن أحمد بن منصور الفقيه: نا أبو بكر أحمد بن علي الحافظ، قال حدثني علي بن أبي علي المعدل قال مات القاضي أبو بكر محمد بن الطيب في يوم السبت السبع بقين من ذي القعدة سنة ثلاث وأربعمائة.

Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a brief biographical notice giving the death date of the Ash'ari scholar al-Baqillani. It provides a short chain of narration and specifies the day (Saturday), the date within the month (with seven days left of Dhu al-Qa'da), and the year (403 AH).

Death Dates of Major Ash'ari Scholars
Ibn Asakir records the death dates of al-Shafi'i, al-Ash'ari, al-Baqillani, and al-Ghazali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the death dates of four major Ash'ari scholars as recorded by Ibn Asakir, and understand how he resolved discrepancies about al-Ash'ari's date.

Core Idea
Ibn Asakir carefully preserved the death dates of key Ash'ari scholars, showing the importance of historical accuracy.

In this passage from Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari, Ibn Asakir provides the death dates of four foundational figures in the Ash'ari tradition: al-Shafi'i, al-Ash'ari, al-Baqillani, and al-Ghazali. He also engages in scholarly critique of conflicting reports about al-Ash'ari's death year.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Let the names and dates stand out.
2
Identify each scholar's death date
List the four scholars and the death dates Ibn Asakir records for them. Note that for al-Ash'ari, he gives multiple opinions before choosing one.
Al-Shafi'i: 204 AH; al-Ash'ari: 324 AH (preferred); al-Baqillani: 403 AH; al-Ghazali: 505 AH.
3
Examine the discussion on al-Ash'ari's date
Notice how Ibn Asakir weighs different reports: one range (320-330 AH), a specific date from Ibn Hazm (324 AH), and a later claim (330-something). He rejects the latter and confirms 324 AH.
He also clarifies that the date 300 AH refers to al-Ash'ari's return to Ahl al-Sunnah, not his death.
4
Reflect on the historical method
Consider why Ibn Asakir included the isnad chains and the clarification. This shows the importance of verifying reports.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سمعت الربيع بن سليمان يقول مات الشافعي في سنة أربع ومائتين في آخر رجب . وأما وفاة أبى الحسن الأشعرى فأخبرنا الشيخان أبو الحسن على ابن أحمد المالكي وأبو منصور محمد بن عبد الملك المقرى قالا قال لنا أبو بكر أحمد بن على الحافظ ذكر لي أبو القسم عبدالواحد بن على الاسدى ان الأشعرى مات ببغداد بعد سنة عشرين وقبل سنة ثلاثين وثلثمائة ودفن في مشرع الزوايا فى تربة إلى جانبها مسجد وبالقرب منها حمام وهي عن يسار المار من السوق إلى دجلة وذكر أبو محمد على بن أحمد ابن سعيد بن حزم الاندلسى ان ابا الحسن الأشعرى مات سنة أربع وعشرين وثلثمائة وقال بعض العصريين مات سنة نيف وثلاثين وثلثمائة وهذا القول الأخير لا أراه صحيحا والأصح أنه مات سنة أربع وعشرين وكذلك ذكر أبو بكر بن فورك فيكون التاريخ سنة ثلثمائة لرجوعه إلى مذهب أهل السنة لا للوقت الذي فيه هلك، وكان رجوعه في حياة الجبائى أبى على وجداله إياه بعد رجوعه من الأمر الجلى، وكانت وفاة الجبائي كما ذكر بعض أهل الاتقان في سنة ثلاث وثلثمائة فى شعبان، وأما وفاة القاضي أبي بكر بن الطيب الباقلاني فأخبرنا أبو الحسن على بن أحمد بن منصور الفقيه نا أبو بكر أحمد بن على الحافظ، قال حدثني على بن أبى على المعدل قال مات القاضى أبو بكر محمد بن الطيب في يوم السبت السبع بقين من ذى القعدة سنة ثلاث وأربعمائة واما وفاة أبى حامد الغزالي فكتب إلى الشيخ أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن اسماعيل بن عبد الغافر الفارسي من نيسابور يذكر انه مضى إلى رحمة الله يوم الاثنين الرابع عشر من جمادى الآخرة سنة خمس وخمسمائة .
I heard al-Rabi' ibn Sulayman say: al-Shafi'i died in the year 204 AH at the end of Rajab. As for the death of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the two shaykhs informed us... that al-Ash'ari died in Baghdad after 320 and before 330 AH and was buried in Mashra' al-Zawaya in a soil next to a mosque, near which is a bathhouse, and it is to the left of the passerby from the market to the Tigris. And Abu Muhammad Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Sa'id ibn Hazm al-Andalusi mentioned that Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari died in the year 324 AH. And some contemporaries said he died in the year 330-something AH, and this last saying I do not consider correct; the correct view is that he died in 324, and Abu Bakr ibn Furak also mentioned that, so the date is 300 AH (he means three hundred?) for his return to the school of Ahl al-Sunnah, not for the time of his death. And his return occurred during the lifetime of al-Jubba'i Abu Ali, and his disputation with him after his return from the clear affair. And the death of al-Jubba'i, as some experts mentioned, was in the year 303 AH in Sha'ban. As for the death of Qadi Abu Bakr ibn al-Tayyib al-Baqillani, ... he died on Saturday, with seven days remaining in Dhu al-Qa'dah of the year 403 AH. And as for the death of Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, ... he passed away to the mercy of Allah on Monday, the fourteenth of Jumada al-Akhirah of the year 505 AH.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a series of biographical notices providing the death dates of four major scholars: al-Shafi'i, al-Ash'ari, al-Baqillani, and al-Ghazali. The author begins with al-Shafi'i's death in 204 AH. For al-Ash'ari, he reports multiple opinions: one that he died between 320 and 330 AH, another from Ibn Hazm specifying 324 AH, and a third from some contemporaries giving around 330 AH. Ibn Asakir prefers 324 AH and cites Ibn Furak in support. He then clarifies that the date 300 AH mentioned by some actually refers to the year of al-Ash'ari's return to Sunni Islam, not his death. He adds that al-Ash'ari's return happened during the lifetime of al-Jubba'i, and that al-Jubba'i died in 303 AH. Next, he gives al-Baqillani's death in 403 AH, and finally al-Ghazali's death in 505 AH. The passage demonstrates Ibn Asakir's care in verifying historical reports and resolving discrepancies.

وفاة
أبو الحسن الأشعري
أبو بكر الباقلاني
أبو حامد الغزالي
الشافعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the date 300 AH refers to al-Ash'ari's death.
Correction: Ibn Asakir explains it refers to his return to Ahl al-Sunnah, not his death.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Hadith on the Generosity of the Ash'aris
Ibn Asakir presents a hadith praising the Ash'aris for their communal sharing during hardship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadith praising the Ash'aris for their communal sharing and how Ibn Asakir uses it to honor Imam al-Ash'ari's lineage.

Core Idea
The Prophet praised the Ash'aris for their selfless sharing — this honor extends to Imam al-Ash'ari.

Imam Ibn Asakir begins this chapter by presenting a hadith that extols the Ash'ari tribe. Through multiple chains of transmission, he establishes the authenticity of this noble virtue. This passage not only highlights the character of the Ash'aris but also serves to elevate the status of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, who belonged to this tribe.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter heading
The heading indicates the purpose: to show the noble origin of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and alert people to his high rank.
2
Examine the chain of transmission
Notice the multiple chains that bring the hadith from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari. The author uses strong, trusted narrators.
The first chain goes through al-Farawi and al-Janzarudhi, the second through al-Khallal and others.
3
Focus on the hadith text
Read the Prophet's words describing how the Ash'aris pool their resources and share equally when food is scarce during travel or at home.
The practice is called 'muwasat' (sharing).
4
Reflect on the Prophet's endorsement
The phrase 'They are from me and I am from them' is a strong statement of belonging and honor.
This indicates a special connection between the Prophet and the tribe.
5
Connect to Imam al-Ash'ari
Understand how this hadith supports the author's aim to honor Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari by linking him to this praised lineage.
The hadith is about the tribe, not the school, but it elevates the founder's origin.

Chapter: Mention of what Abu al-Hasan (may Allah have mercy on him) was granted of noble origin and what came in alerting people of understanding to the greatness of his station in virtue. Praise be to Allah. The two shaykhs informed us ... from Abu Musa (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Indeed the Ash'aris, when they run out of provisions during a military expedition or the food of their families in the city becomes scarce, they gather what they have in a single garment and then divide it equally among themselves in a single vessel. They are from me and I am from them.' ... And another chain: ... The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Indeed the Ash'aris, when they run out of provisions during a military expedition or...'
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading and a series of hadith transmissions. The chapter title indicates that the author intends to mention the noble lineage of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (i.e., his belonging to the Ash'ari tribe) and to alert people of understanding to the greatness of his rank in virtue. The hadith is then cited through multiple chains, all going back to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari. The hadith praises the Ash'ari tribe for their practice of pooling their resources and sharing equally when they face hardship during war or when their families have little food. The Prophet's statement 'They are from me and I am from them' is a strong endorsement. The author's purpose is to link Imam al-Ash'ari to this noble lineage and thereby elevate his status. The multiple chains serve to strengthen the authenticity of the hadith. The passage ends abruptly with the beginning of the second chain's hadith, but the meaning is clear.

Key Terms
الأشعريون
شرف الأصل
أبو موسى الأشعري
الحديث
المواساة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith is about the Ash'ari theological school.
Correction: The hadith refers to the Ash'ari tribe, not the theological school. Ibn Asakir uses it to honor Imam al-Ash'ari as a descendant of the tribe.

Checkpoint
What practice of the Ash'aris does the Prophet praise in this hadith?
A) Their skill in archery
B) Their communal sharing of resources during hardship
C) Their knowledge of jurisprudence
D) Their bravery in battle

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's praise of the Ash'aris' sharing affirms their virtue, and Imam al-Ash'ari's lineage shares in this honor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a shortage—whether of money, time, or resources—do you instinctively protect what is yours, or do you gather with others and share equally? Which of these responses would make the Prophet say about you, 'They are from me and I am from them'?
?
The Ash'aris divided their food using a single vessel, trusting that God would provide. What is the 'single vessel' in your life that you are afraid to share because you doubt it will be enough? Will you take one concrete step this week to share that resource, trusting in God's sufficiency?
?
This hadith honors a tribe for their selflessness in hardship. But you are not from that tribe by blood—so what will you do to earn that same honor through your actions? Will you let the Prophet's praise of them accuse your own stinginess today?

The Double Hijra of the Ash'ari Tribe
Ibn Asakir narrates the hadith of Abu Musa about the Ash'ari emigration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the story of the Ash'ari emigration and the concept of double hijra, and how this hadith highlights the virtue of the Ash'ari tribe.

Core Idea
The Ash'ari tribe earned two emigrations, a unique honor from the Prophet.

This lesson presents a hadith from Imam Ibn Asakir's work on the virtues of the Ash'ari tribe. Abu Musa al-Ash'ari narrates how he and his companions emigrated twice—first to Abyssinia and then to Medina—earning a special share of the Khaybar spoils. This story illustrates the high status of those who undertook both hijras.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith
Read the Arabic hadith and its English translation carefully. Notice the chain of narration and the key details of the story.
Pay attention to how the narrator connects the emigration to the spoils of Khaybar.
2
Identify the double hijra
The Prophet says 'You have two emigrations'. Identify what these two emigrations were: to Abyssinia and to Medina.
3
Reflect on the lesson
Think about why the Ash'ari tribe and Ja'far's group received special treatment. What does this tell us about the virtue of multiple hijras?
This story is often cited to show the excellence of the Ash'ari tribe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو سهل محمد بن إبراهيم الشاهد وأم البهاء فاطمة بنت محمد قالا أنا أبو الفضل عبد الرحمن بن أحمد الرازي أنا جعفر بن عبد الله بن يعقوب نا محمد بن هرون الروياني نا أبو كريب نا أبو أسامة عن بريد عن أبي بردة عن أبي موسى قال خرجنا من اليمن في بضع وخمسين رجلا من قومى، إما قال اثنين وخمسين أو ثلاثة وخمسين ونحن ثلاثة اخوة أبو موسى وأبورهم وأبو عامر فأخرجتنا سفينتنا إلى النجاشي بأرض الجبشة وعنده جعفر بن أبي طالب وأصحابه، فأقبلنا جميعاً في سفينة إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حين افتتح خيبر فما قسم لأحد غاب عن فتح خيبر منها شيء إلا لمن شهد معه إلا لجعفر، وقال ابن سعدويه إلا جعفر وأصحابه أصحاب السفينة قسم لهم معهم وقال لكم الهجرة مرتين حتى هاجرتم إلى النجاشي وهاجرتم إلى ) رواه البخارى ومسلم عن أبى كريب.
Al-Shaykh Abu Sahl Muhammad ibn Ibrahim al-Shahid and Umm al-Baha' Fatima bint Muhammad narrated to us, both saying: Abu al-Fadl Abd al-Rahman ibn Ahmad al-Razi narrated to us, Ja'far ibn Abd Allah ibn Ya'qub narrated to us, Muhammad ibn Harun al-Ruyani narrated to us, Abu Kurayb narrated to us, Abu Usama narrated to us, from Burayd, from Abu Burda, from Abu Musa, who said: We left Yemen with fifty-some men of my people—either he said fifty-two or fifty-three—and we were three brothers: Abu Musa, Abu Ruhm, and Abu Amir. Our ship took us to the Negus in the land of Abyssinia, and with him was Ja'far ibn Abi Talib and his companions. Then we all came together in a ship to the Prophet, peace be upon him, when he conquered Khaybar. He did not distribute any of the spoils to anyone who was absent from the conquest of Khaybar except to those who were present with him, except to Ja'far. Ibn Sa'dawayh said: except Ja'far and his companions, the people of the ship; he gave them a share along with them and said: 'You have two emigrations: you emigrated to the Negus and you emigrated to...' Narrated by al-Bukhari and Muslim from Abu Kurayb.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a hadith narration regarding the emigration of Abu Musa al-Ash'ari and his companions from Yemen. Abu Musa reports that he and about fifty men from his tribe, including his brothers, set out by sea and ended up in Abyssinia where Ja'far ibn Abi Talib was already present. Later they all traveled together to Medina after the conquest of Khaybar. The Prophet distributed spoils from Khaybar only to those present at the battle, but he made an exception for Ja'far and his group (the people of the ship) because they had been in Abyssinia. The Prophet declared that they had performed two emigrations: one to Abyssinia and one to Medina. This hadith highlights the virtue of the Ash'ari tribe (from which Abu Musa and many companions came) and their double hijra, which earned them a special status. The passage ends with the note that al-Bukhari and Muslim narrated it from Abu Kurayb.

Key Terms
هجرة
الأشعريين
أبو موسى
النجاشي
خيبر
جعفر بن أبي طالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Prophet gave Khaybar spoils to all who were absent with a valid excuse.
Correction: The hadith specifies that only Ja'far and his companions received a share because of their double hijra, not all absentees.

Checkpoint
Why did Ja'far and his companions receive a share of the Khaybar spoils despite being absent from the battle?
A) They were relatives of the Prophet.
B) They had performed two emigrations (to Abyssinia and to Medina).
C) They were part of the Ash'ari tribe.
D) They arrived before the battle ended.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari tribe's double hijra earned them special honor from the Prophet, as shown by the exception in the distribution of Khaybar spoils.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have left your home for Allah's sake only once? What prevents you from making that first hijra? If you claim love for Allah, where is your journey?
?
The Prophet honored these people for two emigrations. Are you willing to leave everything - your family, your comfort - twice for the sake of your faith? What holds you back?
?
When you hear about the spoils of Khaybar, do you feel entitled to worldly gains? These people did not even ask; they came after the battle. What is your intention when you seek provision?

The Virtue of Two Migrations: The Story of the Ash'aris
Narration of Ash'aris migrating from Yemen to the Prophet after Khaybar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the exceptional virtue of companions who made multiple migrations, as exemplified by the Ash'ari group led by Ja'far ibn Abi Talib.

Core Idea
Two migrations earned a share of Khaybar's spoils.

This hadith from Abu Musa al-Ash'ari narrates the remarkable journey of a group of Ash'aris from Yemen to Abyssinia and finally to Medina after the conquest of Khaybar. The Prophet's special treatment of Ja'far and his companions highlights the supreme value of emigration for the sake of Allah. Let's explore this inspiring story and its lessons.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Pay attention to the chain of narrators and the key events.
Notice the mention of the Negus and Ja'far.
2
Identify the main characters
List the three brothers: Abu Musa, Abu Ruhm, Abu Amir. Also note Ja'far ibn Abi Talib and the Negus.
3
Understand the exception
The Prophet distributed spoils only to those present at Khaybar, but he made an exception for Ja'far and his companions because of their two migrations.
This shows the high status of those who migrated twice.
4
Reflect on the virtue of hijra
Consider why multiple hijras are so significant in Islam. Emigration is a sacrifice that brings Allah's reward.
The Prophet said: 'You have two migrations.'

أخبرنا الشيخ أبو سهل محمد بن إبراهيم الشاهد وأم البهاء فاطمة بنت محمد قالا أنا أبو الفضل عبد الرحمن بن أحمد الرازي أنا جعفر بن عبد الله بن يعقوب نا محمد بن هرون الروياني، نا أبو كريب نا أبو أسامة عن بريد عن أبي بردة عن أبي موسى قال خرجنا من اليمن في بضع وخمسين رجلا من قومي، إما قال اثنين وخمسين أو ثلاثة وخمسين ونحن ثلاثة اخوة أبو موسى وأبو رهم وأبو عامر فأخرجتنا سفينتنا إلى النجاشي بأرض الحبشة وعنده جعفر بن أبي طالب وأصحابه، فأقبلنا جميعاً في سفينة إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حين افتتح خيبر فما قسم لأحد غاب عن فتح خيبر منها شيء إلا لمن شهد معه إلا لجعفر، وقال ابن سعدويه إلا جعفر وأصحابه أصحاب السفينة قسم لهم معهم وقال لكم الهجرة مرتين حتى هاجرتم إلى النجاشي وهاجرتم إلى ) رواه البخارى ومسلم عن أبى كريب.
Shaykh Abu Sahl Muhammad ibn Ibrahim ash-Shahid and Umm al-Baha Fatima bint Muhammad informed us, both said: Abu al-Fadl Abd ar-Rahman ibn Ahmad ar-Razi informed us: Ja'far ibn Abdullah ibn Ya'qub informed us: Muhammad ibn Harun ar-Ruwani narrated to us: Abu Kurayb narrated to us: Abu Usama narrated from Burayd from Abu Burda from Abu Musa who said: We left Yemen with about fifty men of my people, either he said fifty-two or fifty-three, and we were three brothers: Abu Musa, Abu Ruhm, and Abu Amir. Our ship took us to the Negus in Abyssinia, and with him was Ja'far ibn Abi Talib and his companions. Then we all came in a ship to the Prophet, peace be upon him, when he had conquered Khaybar. He did not distribute anything from Khaybar to anyone who was absent from its conquest except to those who were present with him, except for Ja'far. Ibn Sa'dawayh said: 'Except for Ja'far and his companions, the people of the ship, he gave them a share with them, and he said: You have two migrations: you migrated to the Negus and you migrated (to me).' Narrated by al-Bukhari and Muslim from Abu Kurayb.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This is a hadith narration about the migration of a group of Ash'aris from Yemen. After the conquest of Khaybar, the Prophet distributed spoils only to those who participated, but he made an exception for Ja'far and his companions, who had migrated earlier to Abyssinia. The hadith highlights the virtue of these companions who undertook two migrations (to Abyssinia and to Medina). The isnad includes famous narrators and the hadith is authenticated by al-Bukhari and Muslim.

جعفر بن أبي طالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Prophet gave spoils to all absentees.
Correction: The hadith specifies only Ja'far and his companions received a share because of their two migrations.

Checkpoint
Why was Ja'far ibn Abi Talib given a share of the spoils of Khaybar even though he was absent?
A) He was a close relative of the Prophet.
B) He had made two migrations, including to Abyssinia.
C) He was the leader of the Ash'aris.
D) The Prophet made an exception for all those who had migrated earlier.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet honored the companions who made two migrations by giving them a share of Khaybar's spoils, highlighting the great reward for emigration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Ash'aris left their homes twice for Allah. When was the last time you made a significant sacrifice for your faith? Are you willing to do it again—and again—even if it costs you comfort or status?
?
If you were in the place of the Ash'aris, would you have the courage to leave your family and homeland not once but twice for Allah? What is holding you back from making that kind of commitment today?
?
The Prophet honored them with a share they did not earn by presence. What 'unearned' blessings has Allah given you because of sacrifices you made for Him? And what one sacrifice can you start today to earn more of His mercy?
